- **f6-h5**: Moves the knight to the rim, but it is entirely safe here and functionally pressures f4. Also, the d4 pawn remains safe since `Qe4` doesn't attack h5, allowing you to easily defend d4 with `...Nc6` on the next move.

`f6h5` safely preserves the piece and exploits White's overextension.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position shows that your strong central pawn on d4 is under severe pressure from White's pawn on e3 and Knight on f3, while only being defended by your Knight on c6. If you do nothing, White is threatening to play exd4 (or cxd4) and win the pawn.

However, you have a beautiful tactical resource that uses this pawn to your advantage:

**1... Bb4+**

By bringing your Bishop to b4, you put the White King in check and force a response, which leads to highly favorable tactical scenarios because of the tension around d4:

- If White tries to block the check with **2. Nc3**, your attacked pawn becomes the attacker: **2... dxc3!** winning the Knight for a pawn (if 3. bxc3, you just move the Bishop).
- If White blocks with the Bishop **2. Bd2**, you can safely execute **2... dxe3!**. Now White cannot play 3. Bxe3 because the Bishop is pinned to the King! If White tries to break the pin with 3. Bxb4, you have the devastating **3... exf2+!** where the pawn forks the King and grabs a key pawn, ruining White's position completely (and if 4. Kd2/Kf1 fxg1=Q). If White plays 3. fxe3, you can comfortably trade Bishops with 3... Bxd2+ and you've cleanly won a pawn and shattered White's center.
- If White blocks with a Knight via **2. Nbd2**, you still play **2... dxe3 3. fxe3**, cleanly trading the doomed pawn and leaving White's d2 Knight awkwardly pinned.
- If White blocks with **2. Nfd2**, moving the f3 Knight, your d4 pawn is no longer outnumbered and becomes completely safe, leaving you with a crushing space advantage.

Developing the Bishop with tempo is the perfect way to seize the initiative and solve the problem of the hanging d4 pawn.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent position for Black. Let's analyze the situation and find the best continuation.

**Evaluation:**
You are currently **up a full pawn**. It appears earlier in the game your c-pawn captured White's d-pawn, leading to your strong advanced pawn on `d4`. White has just played `e2-e3`, directly attacking your extra `d4` pawn in an attempt to win their material back.

**Candidate Moves:**
1. **... O-O (`e8g8`)**: A solid developing move, but this allows White to play `exd4`, recovering their lost pawn and equalizing the material.
2. **... d3 (`d4d3`)**: Pushing the pawn to escape the attack. It hits the White Queen on `c2`, but White simply responds with `Qxd3`. Since the `d3` square is completely undefended by your pieces, this just blunders the pawn back for free. 
3. **... dxe3 (`d4e3`)**: Capturing the attacking pawn. This is the principled response! 

**Why `d4e3` is the strongest move:**
By capturing on `e3`, you maintain your +1 material advantage. White is essentially forced to recapture with `fxe3` (since the Queen on `c2` cannot move there, and the `c1` Bishop is blocked by the pinned `d2` Knight). 

If White plays `fxe3`, you achieve several strategic victories:
- You keep your extra pawn.
- You severely damage White's pawn structure, leaving them with an awkward center.
- You open up lines against White's King which is currently stuck in the center (`e1`), especially considering their `d2` Knight is painfully pinned by your `b4` Bishop. 

With White's position weakened, you'll be able to comfortably castle kingside (`O-O`) on the next turn and enjoy a dominant, materially winning position.

make_move d4e3

The opening has left White with an unusual structure, highlighted by the missing d-pawn and f-pawn, and an overextended pawn on g5. Given the status of the board, the most principled and sound approach is to complete kingside development. Castling gets the King out of the center and onto safety, while activating the h8 Rook. White's overextended pawn on g5 currently poses no immediate threat to the castled king—in fact, the h5 Knight anchors the defense perfectly, preventing any easy infiltration on the h-file. Attempting to force matters prematurely (e.g. by taking with the bishop on d2 or grabbing squares with the Queen) would only relinquish the tension and the pin we favorably hold. 

Therefore, standard Bogo/Nimzo-Indian principles apply perfectly here: prioritize king safety and complete development before launching an attack on the center or exploiting White's overextended pawns.

After `axb4`, White has given up their key dark-squared bishop (which is crucial for defending the light squares around the king), and the a-file is ripped wide open for your rook anyway. 

It's a fast, forcing, and highly effective pawn sacrifice (though technically, Black is currently up a pawn, so you are just giving it back to destroy White's king cover).

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the analysis of the position, you have a strong central break available. 

Currently, White is putting pressure on the queenside and has launched a pawn storm on the kingside with `g5`. Your knight successfully blockades the h-file from `h5`, rendering White's kingside attack much slower and largely ineffective for the time being.

You have the opportunity to seize the initiative in the center with **d6-d5**. This highly thematic Sicilian central strike is incredibly strong here for several reasons:
1. **The Fork Threat**: Pushing `d5` immediately threatens `d5-d4` on the next move. A pawn on `d4` would brutally fork White's Bishop on `c3` and the pawn on `e3`.
2. **Total Safety**: The `d5` square is completely safe. White has no pawns that can capture on `d5`, and any capture by White's Queen (`Qxd5`) would simply lose the Queen to your `e6` pawn (`exd5`).
3. **Space & Tempo**: To stop the `d4` fork, White will be forced to respond passively (such as playing `Bd4` or `e4`). If White plays `Bd4` to blockade, you can kick the bishop immediately with `e5`, gaining even more space. If White plays `e4`, you can simply capture `dxe4`, blowing the center wide open while White's King on `c1` remains vulnerable.

This move forces White's hand and dictates the pace of the game in your favor.

2.  **Evaluate 1... Kf8 (g8f8):**
    *   If I move my King to `f8`, White has the devastating follow-up `2. Rf1+`.
    *   The `f`-file is completely open for the White Rook. My King has to move. It cannot go to `g8` or `e8` or `g7` because the White Queen on `g6` controls those squares (e.g., `g6` to `e8` is a diagonal, `g6` to `g8`/`g7` is vertical).
    *   So, my King is forced to `e7` (`2... Ke7`).
    *   White continues the attack with `3. Rf7+`. This check is protected by the White Queen on `g6` (diagonal `g6-f7`).
    *   From `e7`, my King cannot go to `e8` (covered by Queen), `f8/f6` (covered by Rook), `e6` (occupied by my own pawn), or `d6` (occupied by my own Rook). Because the Rook on `f7` controls the 7th rank, `d7` is also inaccessible.
    *   Therefore, the King is forced to `d8` (`3... Kd8`).
    *   Then, White delivers checkmate with `4. Qg8#`. The Queen checks along the 8th rank. `c8` is occupied by my Bishop, `c7/d7/e7` are cut off by the `f7` Rook, and no piece can block the check on `e8` or `f8`.
    *   *Conclusion for `Kf8`:* It leads to a forced checkmate in 3 moves for White.

3.  **Evaluate 1... Kh8 (g8h8):**
    *   If I move my King to `h8`, White's most forcing continuation is `2. Qh6+` (forcing `2... Kg8`) followed by `3. Rg1+`.
    *   After `3. Rg1+`, my King is forced out to `f7` (`3... Kf7`), as `f8` and `h8` are covered by the White Queen.
    *   White can continue checking with `4. Rg7+` or `4. Qh7+`.
    *   If `4. Qh7+`, I can step to `e8` (`4... Ke8`). If White then plays `5. Rg8+`, my King safely escapes to the queenside via `d7` (`5... Kd7`) and then to `c6`.
    *   If `4. Rg7+`, I can also step to `e8` (`4... Ke8`), and after `5. Qg8+ Kd7 6. Rg7+ Kc6`, my King runs all the way to the queenside where White has no pieces to continue the attack.
    *   *Conclusion for `Kh8`:* The King successfully escapes the mating net by running towards the center and the queenside, surviving the attack.

4.  **Final Decision:** `g8h8` is the only move that does not lead to a forced checkmate.

I am ready to make the move.

Clearly, `g7f6` is the only move that escapes the mating sequence and keeps Black in the game.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board reveals a closed, reversed Benoni-like pawn structure where Black enjoys a significant space advantage in the center thanks to the pawn on d4. White has just played `a3`, asking a direct question to our dark-squared Bishop on b4. 

Let's evaluate our options for the Bishop:
1. **Retreating to a5 (Ba5)**: This allows White to immediately launch a queenside expansion with b2-b4. Following Bb6, White traps the bishop with c4-c5.
2. **Retreating to d6 (Bd6)**: This steps straight into the path of White's c-pawn, allowing c4-c5 with tempo, forcing us to move the bishop yet again and cementing a space advantage for White.
3. **Retreating to e7 (Be7)**: While solid, the e7 square unfortunately blocks our own Rook on e8. Currently, the e-file is completely wide open, and our Rook on e8 is absolutely pinning White's passive Bishop on e2 to the King on e1. Blocking this file voluntarily relieves a huge amount of pressure from White's position.
4. **Capturing on d2 (Bxd2+)**: The White Knight on d2 is actually pinned to the uncastled White King on e1. Capturing it comes with check, forcing White to recapture immediately (with either the Queen on d1 or the Bishop on c1). 

Positionally, our dark-squared Bishop is considered our "bad" bishop because most of our central pawns (c7, d4, f7) are fixed on light squares. Trading it off for a piece that could otherwise maneuver to useful squares like b3 or e4 is strategically beneficial. Additionally, after the trade, White's King remains stuck in the center with the e2 Bishop awkwardly pinned to it by our e8 Rook.

This move perfectly addresses the immediate threat, improves our minor piece arrangement, and keeps White's position under maximum tension.

I will proceed with capturing the Knight.
_UCI Move:_ b4d2.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent choice in this position is developing your light-squared bishop to put immediate pressure on White's setup and exploit the pin on the e-file. 

Let's break down why **Bg4** is extremely strong here:
1. **Exploiting the Pin:** Your rook on e8 is directly pinning White's light-squared bishop on e2 to their king on e1. This means the e2 bishop is paralyzed and cannot move off the e-file. Because of this, White's queen on d1 is also blocked from seeing the g4 square. You can safely place your bishop on g4 without it being captured.
2. **Preventing Development:** White desperately needs to develop their g1 knight to f3 to prepare for kingside castling. However, placing your bishop on g4 completely deters `Nf3`. If White tries it, you can simply capture the knight (`Bxf3`), and because the e2 bishop is pinned, White would be forced to recapture with their g-pawn (`gxf3`). This would completely shatter their kingside pawn structure and make castling suicidal. 
3. **Seizing the Initiative:** By playing Bg4, you force White to either make awkward pawn moves like `f3` or `h3` (which creates weaknesses) or suffer with a paralyzed kingside, keeping their king stranded in the center where your e8 rook commands the open file.

I am ready to make the move.

Let's evaluate the options for the bishop:
- `f5g6`: Moves into the attacking radius of White's knight on h4 (`Nxg6`). 
- `f5g4`: Steps onto a square controlled by White's light-squared bishop on e2 (`Bxg4`).
- `f5e4`: Blunders the bishop to White's d3 pawn (`dxe4`).
- `f5d3`: Captures a pawn but loses the bishop since d3 is heavily defended by White's queen and e2 bishop (`Qxd3` or `Bxd3`).

Securing the bishop by moving it to the safe h3 outpost maintains your material and positional advantage.

1. **Board Evaluation**: 
You are playing as Black in what strongly resembles a King's Indian Defense structure. You are doing well materially, and the pawn structure has locked up much of the center. White has a pawn on d3 that is severely backward and unsupported by other pawns (there's no e-pawn, and the c-pawn has already advanced to c4).

2. **Evaluating Options**:
- **a5xb4**: Opening the a-file is a common theme to challenge White's queenside expansion, but it’s a non-forcing exchange.
- **Nxg5 (e6g5)**: At first glance, the g5 pawn looks undefended, but checking the diagonals shows that White's dark-squared bishop on d2 is perfectly eyeing that pawn (d2-e3-f4-g5). Capturing it would immediately blunder your knight to `Bxg5`.
- **Ne5 (c6e5)**: This is a textbook positional and tactical shot. By jumping your knight from c6 to e5, you place it on an eternal, unassailable outpost. Because White has no f-pawn and the d-pawn is pinned back on d3, no pawn will ever be able to chase this knight away. 

3. **The Tactical Threat of c6e5**: 
Moving the knight to e5 carries a massive immediate threat. The knight on e5 directly attacks the completely undefended `d3` pawn. If White ignores this to push a queenside agenda (like `bxa5`), you have the crushing `Nxd3+!`, winning a central pawn with a royal fork/check against the White King resting safely on f2. White is practically forced to make a passive move to defend the d3-pawn (such as `Bc1`), handing the initiative completely over to you. 

I am ready to finalize your turn.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, let's carefully evaluate the current position:

1. **Material Advantage**: 
You (Black) and White both have two Rooks and a minor piece (your Knight vs White's Bishop). However, you have a staggering 6 pawns against White's 2. Being up 4 pawns gives you a massive, completely winning material advantage. Your primary goal should be to consolidate this advantage, avoid blunders, and restrict any counterplay.

2. **Immediate Threats**: 
Looking closely at White's last move (King to f1), there is a direct and sneaky threat. Your active Rook on e2 is undefended and stands diagonally adjacent to the White King on f1. The White King is currently attacking your Rook! Since the Rook is completely unprotected, White is threatening to simply play `Kxe2` on the next turn, effectively winning a full Rook for free and destroying your dominant position. 

3. **Candidate Moves**:
- **`g7g6` or `d3e5`**: While these are tempting moves to kick White's active Rook on f5, they completely ignore the hanging Rook on e2. Playing them would result in White happily capturing your Rook (`Kxe2`), turning a won game into a nightmare. 
- Moving the Rook away (e.g., **`e2h2`** winning a pawn or retreating to **`e2e6`**): Moving the Rook escapes the threat, but it gives the White King breathing room to escape its confinement on the 1st rank. The `Re2` and `Nd3` combined form a brutal cage around White's pieces. 
- **`a8e8` (Rae8)**: This brilliant developing move perfectly solves the problem. By bringing your last inactive piece to the open e-file, you firmly defend the attacked Rook on e2. 

**Why `a8e8` is best:**
- **Saves the piece**: A King cannot capture a defended piece, rendering the White King's threat on e2 completely neutralized.
- **Maintains the Bind**: Your Rook stays right where it hurts White the most. Combined with your centralized Knight on d3, White's King is forever boxed in (it cannot step to the `e` file, and `g2` is covered). Furthermore, White's Rook on a1 is completely paralyzed.
- **Positional Dominance**: Doubling the Rooks on the central open file maximizes your control of the board. With your pieces harmonized and safe, you can comfortably push your extra pawns or launch a final attack on the next moves.

I am ready to make the move.

make_move a8e8

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, let's analyze the current situation and the threats on the board.

**Evaluation of the Position:**
White has just played `Qxb7`, capturing your b-pawn. The White Queen is now aggressively placed on `b7` and directly attacks your undefended `a8` Rook. 

Your most pressing issue is saving the Rook on `a8` or trapping the overextended White Queen.

**Candidate Moves:**
1. **... Bc6 (`d7c6`)** 
At first glance, moving the Bishop to `c6` looks incredibly strong because it immediately attacks the White Queen and seemingly covers `a8` (meaning `Qxa8` would be met by `...Bxa8`). 
However, this falls into a devastating tactical trap! If you play `1... Bc6`, White will respond with `2. Bb5!`. This absolutely pins your `c6` Bishop to your King on `e8`. Because the Bishop is pinned, it can no longer capture the Queen on `b7` or `a8`. You will end up losing massive material (e.g., `2... Qd7 3. Qxa8 Bxa8 4. Bxd7+ Nxd7`, where you lose a Rook for a Bishop).

2. **... Nc6 (`b8c6`)**
By developing the Knight to `c6`, you achieve several crucial things at once:
- You vacate the `b8` square. Why is this important? Because it clears the 8th rank, allowing your Queen on `d8` to look across and **defend the `a8` Rook**. 
- If White blindly continues with `2. Qxa8`, you simply play `2... Qxa8` and win the Queen for a Rook.
- You actively develop a piece toward the center. 
- You prepare the follow-up `...Rb8` on your next turn. The move `...Rb8` will attack the White Queen, forcing it to retreat and completely taking over the initiative.
- If White tries to pin the Knight with `2. Bb5`, it poses no threat because you can still safely play `2... Rb8`. If White takes the Knight `3. Bxc6+`, your Bishop on `d7` is perfectly placed to recapture with `3... Bxc6`, regaining a solid position and still forcing the White Queen away.

Move `...Na6` (`b8a6`) also unblocks the 8th rank to defend the Rook but develops the Knight to a worse square on the rim, and hilariously blunders the Knight to `2. Bxa6!` where recapturing it with the Rook (`...Rxa6`) leads to `3. Qxa6`, losing a full piece and the exchange.

Therefore, `...Nc6` is the only move that properly defends your position, develops a piece, and punishes White's greedy Queen excursion.

make_move b8c6

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the position reveals that White is currently threatening to win a pawn and create severe problems on your queenside. 

White's Queen on a4 is lined up directly against your a7 pawn. Since your Rook has moved to b6, the a7 pawn is left completely undefended. If you ignore this (for instance, by playing a developing move like `e7e6`), White will simply play `Qxa7`, winning a pawn and infiltrating your position. 

You might be tempted to immediately kick the Bishop with `a7a6`, which is a standard idea in these structures. However, in this specific position, that would be a catastrophic blunder! If you play `a7a6`, White responds with `Bxa6!`. Because your Rook on b6 defends the a6 square, you can recapture with `Rxa6`, but then White follows up with `Qxa6`. Because the a-file is open, White's Queen safely captures the Rook. You would end up losing a Rook and a pawn for a Bishop.

Moving the Rook to defend the pawn (`b6b7`) is also a fatal trap. The b6 Rook is currently performing a vital defensive duty: it defends your c6 Knight horizontally. If the Rook moves to b7, the Knight is only defended by your d7 Bishop, but it is attacked by *two* White pieces (the a4 Queen and the b5 Bishop). White would play `Bxc6`, and after `Bxc6`, White plays `Qxc6+`, winning a piece and forking your King and the b7 Rook!

To solve all of these problems simultaneously, you need a move that defends the a7 pawn, maintains the defense of c6, and prepares to untangle your position. 

The most precise and brilliant move here is to slide your Queen into the corner.

**make_move d8a8**

Putting the Queen on a8 seems unusual at first glance, but it serves multiple profound purposes:
1. **Defends a7:** The Queen protects the pawn vertically, completely stopping `Qxa7` (since `Qxa7 Qxa7` wins the Queen).
2. **Overprotects c6:** From a8, the Queen looks down the long diagonal and adds a *third* layer of defense to the c6 Knight, rendering any sacrifices by White completely useless.
3. **Sets a deadly trap:** By putting the Queen on the a-file, you are preparing to play `a7a6` on your next turn! Once you play `a6`, the a-file will open up, meaning your Queen on a8 will defend the a6 square. If White tries the `Bxa6` trick then, after `Rxa6 Qxa6`, you will simply play `Qxa6`, winning the White Queen!

More importantly, our Bishop on c6 will immediately attack White's Queen on a4! White will be forced to waste a turn retreating their Queen (e.g., to c2 or d1) because trading their Queen for our Bishop (Qxc6+) would blunder their Queen. 

By playing `d7c6`, we restore material equality, develop our Bishop to an incredibly active square (where it also eyes the f3 Knight), and gain a free tempo.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, we have to carefully analyze the position and evaluate the threats.

Currently, you are down a significant amount of material (a rook, knight, and several pawns). However, White is threatening an immediate game-ending sequence. 

Notice White's Queen on **e6**. If you aren't careful, White wants to play **Qe8#**. 
Let's look at why that is mate if allowed:
- White's rook on **e1** strongly supports the e-file. If the White Queen lands on e8, it is fully protected by the e1-Rook, meaning your King on f8 cannot capture it.
- Your King on f8 has no escape squares: f7 and e7 are covered by the Queen, and g8 is also covered along the 8th rank (as you cannot step back along the line of attack). g7 is occupied by your own Bishop.
- Any attempt to block the check is impossible since the Queen would deliver it from an adjacent square (e8 to f8).

Currently, your Queen on **a8** is the unsung hero keeping you alive! By staying anywhere on the 8th rank (a8, b8, c8, or d8), your Queen keeps an eye on e8 and is prepared to capture the White Queen if she dares to step there (`Qe8+ Qxe8`), delaying the mate and forcing a trade instead. Furthermore, keeping your Queen specifically on **a8** allows it to also protect your **a6** pawn simultaneously from being picked off by White's Queen (`Qxa6`).

Because any aggressive Queen excursions like `a8f3` (trying to pick off the undefended f3 pawn) instantly blunder the game to `Qe8#`, your Queen is tied down to defensive duty. With no other active plans that improve your material deficit without immediate repercussions, making a solid, improving waiting pawn move is your best option to force White to show their hand. 

`h7h6` is a safe prophylactic move that solidifies your kingside structure and doesn't create any new weaknesses.

make_move h7h6

The current position is difficult for Black, as you are significantly down in material (missing a Rook, a Knight, and several pawns compared to White). Your primary goal right now is to stabilize the position, avoid immediate checkmates, and not give away any more material for free.

Let's evaluate the position:
1. **The Mate Threat:** White's Queen is aggressively centralized on `e6`. Because the `e`-file is half-open, White constantly threatens to bring their Queen to `e8` (supported by the `e1` Rook if the Queen moves there), which would be a devastating mate. However, your Queen on `a8` currently perfectly covers the 8th rank, making `e8` fully protected by your Queen. Moving your Queen off the 8th rank (such as `a8f3`, `a8d5`, or `a8b7`) removes this vital defense and immediately allows a back-rank checkmate (`Qe8#`). 
2. **The Pawn Attack:** White's Queen on `e6` is horizontally attacking your pawn on `a6`. Currently, your Queen on `a8` is the *only* piece defending the `a6` pawn. This means your Queen is tied down; any safe Queen move along the 8th rank (like `a8d8` or `a8c8`) abandons the pawn and drops it for free (`Qxa6`).

To resolve this and untangle your pieces without blundering, the most principled and safe continuation is to push the `a`-pawn to `a5`. 

By playing `a6a5`:
- You move the pawn to a safe square, stepping out of the `e6` Queen's horizontal line of attack.
- You free your Queen from its passive defensive duty of protecting `a6`. On subsequent turns, your Queen can become active along the 8th rank (such as moving to `d8` to control the `d`-file and prevent checks like `Qd6+`) while continuing to guard against `Qe8#`.
- You don't hang any material and avoid creating any new weaknesses in front of your King.

make_move a6a5

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The Black King on e7 is currently in check by the White Bishop on a3. 

We have four legal moves to resolve this check:
1. `d8d6`: Blocks the check with the Rook. However, the d6 square is heavily controlled by White's Knight on c4 and Rook on d4. Blocking with the Rook just throws it away for free (e.g., White plays `Nxd6+` or `Rxd6+` and the capturing piece will be protected by the other, meaning we couldn't even recapture). 
2. `e7f7`: Moves the King to f7.
3. `e7e6`: Moves the King to e6.
4. `e7e8`: Moves the King to e8.

To choose the best King move, we must look at the rest of the board. Importantly, White's Rook on d4 is currently attacking our only Rook on d8. 
- If we play `e7f7` or `e7e6`, we move our King away from the d8 Rook, leaving it completely undefended. White will happily play `Rxd8`, winning our Rook for absolutely nothing.
- If we play `e7e8`, our King slides over and maintains defense of the d8 Rook. While White can still play `Rxd8+`, we will at least be able to recapture with `Kxd8`, forcing an equal trade of rooks rather than dropping a full piece. 

Given our material disadvantage, avoiding giving away our last major piece for free is paramount. 

Therefore, moving the King to e8 is forced. 

Action: `make_move e7e8`

Currently, your king on f7 is in check by the White knight on e5. You have a few options: moving the king to e8, e6, g7, g8, or capturing the knight with your f6 pawn (`f6e5`). 

Let's evaluate the consequences:
1. **King Moves (e.g., `f7e8` or `f7g7`)**: If you move your king away, White's d4 rook is directly attacking your undefended d8 rook. Furthermore, moving your king out of the way of the e-file lines it up with White's e1 rook. This means White's knight on e5 could later move with a devastating discovered check (e.g., `Nd3+`), allowing White to save their knight AND capture your d8 rook for free. You would lose a whole rook without any compensation.
2. **Capturing the Knight (`f6e5`)**: By capturing the knight immediately, you remove the checking piece and pocket 3 points of material. In response, White will likely play `Rxd8` to take your rook. However, notice that once your pawn lands on e5, it double-attacks White's d4 rook along with your d8 rook. White is practically forced into this rook trade to avoid losing more material. By doing this, you've essentially traded your rook for a knight, dropping an exchange rather than losing a full piece for absolutely nothing.

Capturing the knight is by far the most principled and resilient continuation.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, we need to carefully evaluate the consequences of moving your King to each of the available squares: **h7**, **f7**, or **h6**.

Your King is currently on g6 and is in check from White's Rook on g5. White has a massive material advantage (two Rooks and a Bishop), so our immediate goal is to find the most resilient move that avoids tactical blunders, immediate pins, or discovered checks.

Let's look at the options:
1. **g6f7 (Kf7):** 
Moving the King to f7 places it on the 7th rank alongside White's Rook on d7 and Bishop on e7. This is a fatal mistake because it allows a devastating discovered check. On the next turn, White can simply move their e7 Bishop (e.g., to d6 or f6), unleashing the d7 Rook to check your King. After you are forced to move the King away, White will freely capture your g7 Bishop with their g5 Rook. 

2. **g6h7 (Kh7):** 
Moving the King to h7 also keeps it on the 7th rank. While there is no immediate discovered check (because your g7 Bishop is in the way), it allows White to execute a deadly pin. White can play a move like 2. Bd6. With the e7 Bishop gone, White's d7 Rook will be staring down the 7th rank right at your King, absolutely pinning your g7 Bishop to it. Simultaneously, the g5 Rook is attacking the g7 Bishop. Because it is pinned, your Bishop cannot flee. Since you have no other pieces to defend it, White will capture your Bishop on the next move.

3. **g6h6 (Kh6):**
This is the only saving move. By moving to the 6th rank, your King completely steps out of the dangerous 7th rank alignments. 
If White tries the same trick with 2. Bd6 to attack your g7 Bishop with both Rooks, your Bishop is **not pinned** (since your King is safely on h6). This gives you the freedom to simply move your Bishop to safety (for example, to f6). 
Furthermore, if White attempts to get fancy with a discovered check like 2. Bf8+, this move forces their Bishop to abandon the defense of their g5 Rook, allowing your King to simply capture it with 2... Kxg5! 

Moving to h6 completely neutralizes White's immediate tactical threats and saves your Bishop.

**make_move g6h6**

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the board reveals that your dark-squared Bishop on c5 is currently hanging, being attacked by White's Rook on g5. You must either move it or defend it.

However, White also has a Bishop on a5 that is currently undefended. You can exploit this situation with a powerful pawn push:

**b7b6**

Here's why this move is crushing:
1. **It defends your attacked Bishop:** By moving to b6, your pawn now defends the c5 Bishop. If White decides to capture it with the Rook (`Rxc5`), you can simply recapture with your new b6 pawn (`bxc5`), winning a Rook for a Bishop (a favorable exchange).
2. **It traps White's Bishop:** Your b6 pawn immediately attacks White's undefended Bishop on a5. 
3. **White has no escape:** If you look at the escape squares for White's Bishop on the a5-e1 diagonal, every single one is completely covered by your pieces!
   - `b4` is covered by your c5 Bishop.
   - `c3` is covered by your d4 pawn.
   - `d2` is covered by your e2 Rook.
   - `e1` is covered by your e2 Rook.

White cannot move the Bishop to safety without it being captured. If White tries to save it by defending it with a pawn push (`b2-b4`), they will attack your c5 Bishop again, but you can safely retreat it (e.g., to e7) and White's a5 Bishop remains hopelessly trapped by your pawn, to be captured on the following turn. 

You are guaranteed to win at least a piece for a pawn.

make_move b7b6

The position is an endgame where Black has a significant positional advantage and active pieces. Let's analyze the targets and piece activity.

White's g2 pawn is currently under pressure from your e2 Rook along the second rank. Currently, White's only defender of the g2 pawn is the g5 Rook (which defends down the open g-file). 

By playing the Bishop to c6, you unleash a powerful dual-purpose move:
1. **Double Attack:** The Bishop on c6 targets the g2 pawn along the long c6-g2 diagonal (c6-d5-e4-f3-g2). With both your c6 Bishop and e2 Rook now attacking g2, and only the g5 Rook defending it, White is faced with a massive, immediate threat to win a pawn and completely collapse their kingside.
2. **Restriction:** The c6 Bishop also beautifully covers the critical d5 square. Because White's g5 Rook is extremely short on safe squares (as your pawns and pieces control almost all adjacent squares on the 5th rank and g-file), taking away d5 prepares a devastating trap. If White tries to save the g2 pawn (for example by playing `g2-g3`), you can follow up with `c5-e7` on the next turn, attacking the g5 Rook and leaving it completely paralyzed.

White is forced into a corner and will likely have to compromise their pawn structure or give up material.

**Current Position:**
* **White:** King on d3, Bishop on g3.
* **Black:** King on d7, Bishop on g2, Pawns on a7, c7, c3, d4, h7, h5.
Black is enjoying a massive material advantage (up a Bishop and 6 pawns vs. 0 pawns).

**White's Threat:**
Your incredibly dangerous pawn on c3 is currently protected by your d4 pawn. However, the d4 pawn itself is entirely *unprotected*, and the White King on d3 is directly attacking it. If White is allowed to play `Kxd4`, not only do you lose a central pawn, but the crucial protection for your c3 pawn instantly vanishes, meaning `Kxc3` would follow shortly after. 

**Candidate Moves:**
1. **c3-c2:** This might look tempting to push the pawn closer to promotion. However, since it leaves the protective diagonal of your d4 pawn, White's King will simply capture it for free with `Kxc2`.
2. **Bg2-e4+:** You could check the King, but this forces `Kxe4`. While you could then push `c2`, White will follow up with `Bf4`, covering the c1 promotion square. You'd eventually promote by sacrificing your pawn for the Bishop, but you would be needlessly giving up your own Bishop and an advanced pawn when you don't have to.
3. **c7-c5!:** This is the most principled and crushing move. By pushing your c7 pawn two squares, you instantly create an impregnable pawn chain: **c5 protects d4, and d4 protects c3**. 

By playing `c5`, the White King is completely stymied. It can no longer capture `d4`, and it remains unable to capture `c3`. White's pieces will be completely paralyzed, allowing you to easily bring your King down the board or push your h-pawns to cement an effortless victory without sacrificing a single piece of material.

The White King on `d3` is currently blockaded by this pawn chain and restricted from moving forward, while White's dark-squared Bishop on `f4` has no immediate targets that aren't well-defended.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To decide on the best move, we need to look at the interactions between your pawns, your bishop, and the trapped White king. 

Currently, White's king is practically paralyzed on c4. It is surrounded by your pawns but is eyeing your c3 pawn. Right now, your c3 pawn is safely guarded by the d4 pawn, which in turn is protected by the c5 pawn (which is defended by your King on c6). 

However, we want to march these pawns down the board to guarantee a victory. If you play `d4d3` right away, the c3 pawn would lose its protection and White's king could simply capture it (`Kxc3`). 

The most elegant and crushing move here is to push the c-pawn:
**1... c3-c2**

Here is why this move is overwhelmingly strong:
1. **Perfect Synergy:** When the pawn lands on c2, it steps directly onto the light-squared diagonal (`e4-d3-c2-b1`) controlled by your own Bishop on e4. This means the pawn is perfectly safe and White's King cannot capture it. 
2. **Paving the Way:** By advancing the c-pawn, you vacate the c3 square. On the next move, you will be able to play `d4-d3`. Once you do that, the d3 pawn will be protected by your e4 Bishop, and the d3 pawn will simultaneously protect the c2 pawn!
3. **Total Domination:** With the unbreakable pawn chain established on c2 and d3, White's King will be permanently locked out and rendered useless. You can then simply march your h-pawn down the board (`h5-h4-h3-h2-h1=Q`) without White's dark-squared bishop being able to stop it, as h1 is a light square.

make_move c3c2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

This is a great tactical position. Let's analyze the checks available to Black:

1. **e4d5+** 
If you check with the Bishop on d5, you attack the White King on c4. The King's flight squares b5, d5 are covered by your King on c6, b4 is covered by your a5 pawn, and d4/c3 are covered by your Queen. However, moving the Bishop away from e4 abandons its defense of your d3 pawn (along the e4-d3 diagonal). White is forced to play **Kd3**, capturing your pawn. While you can then follow up with **Kxc5** to win the White Bishop, you had to sacrifice a pawn to do it.

2. **a1a4+** 
This is the cleanest and most punishing move. By swinging the Queen up the a-file, you deliver check to the White King along the 4th rank.
- b3 and d4 are cut off by the Queen.
- b4 is covered by both the Queen and your a5 pawn.
- b5 and d5 remain covered by your King. 
- Crucially, your Bishop safely remains on e4, meaning your **d3 pawn stays protected**. 

White only has two legal replies to this check:
- **Kc3**: The King steps out of the check, but doing so abandons the defense of his own Bishop. You can immediately play **Kxc5**, winning the piece for absolutely nothing. 
- **Bc5-b4**: White tries to block the check with the Bishop. But because the b4 square is controlled by your a5 pawn, you can simply capture the Bishop with **a5xb4**. White's King is forced to step away (Kc3) as the pawn is guarded by the Queen, netting you a free piece. 

In every variation, **a1a4+** wins the Bishop completely for free without surrendering any material in return, leading to an easily won endgame.

make_move a1a4

The board indicates that White's Bishop has just captured a piece on f6. Black must recapture the Bishop to maintain material parity. The two legal ways to recapture are with the e-pawn (`e7f6`) or the g-pawn (`g7f6`). 

Capturing with the e-pawn (`e7f6`) is the classical and generally preferred choice in such positions (like the Trompowsky Attack or similar structures). It opens up the f8-a3 diagonal for the rapid development of the dark-squared Bishop and reinforces central control. While it results in doubled pawns on the f-file (f6 and f7), the benefits of fluid development and a solid center far outweigh this structural minor flaw. Capturing with the g-pawn (`g7f6`) would open the g-file for the rook but leave the dark-squared Bishop somewhat locked in, making development more awkward.

Therefore, `e7f6` is the strongest and most natural continuation. I will proceed with this capture.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To determine the best move, let's analyze the current position and threats:
1. White's King is currently on `b3` and is directly attacking your unprotected Knight on `c4`. 
2. If you don't move the Knight or defend it, White will simply play `Kxc4` and win your piece. 
3. Moving the Knight to `b6` (`c4b6`) is the most precise and solid continuation. 

By playing `c4b6`, not only do you save the Knight by moving it to a safe square, but from `b6`, the Knight casts a perfect net over the `a4` and `c4` squares. With your `b4` pawn covering `a3` and `c3` (and the pawn itself firmly guarded by your Bishop on `c5`), the White King is entirely blocked from stepping forward onto the 4th rank and is forced to retreat backwards to `a2`, `b2`, or `c2`. This entirely shuts down any queenside counterplay from White, securing your overwhelming material advantage for an easy endgame conversion.

make_move c4b6

From e5, the Queen already takes away the crucial escape squares on the 5th rank (b5, c5, d5) as well as the diagonals (d6, c7), drastically limiting the White King's available moves to just b6, b7, and d7.
